	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Anglo-Saxons</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Anglo-Saxon (disambiguation)">Anglo-Saxon (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Germanic tribes who started to inhabit parts of Great Britain from the 5th century onwards</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LindisfarneChiRiho.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/LindisfarneChiRiho.jpg/300px-LindisfarneChiRiho.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="419" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/LindisfarneChiRiho.jpg/450px-LindisfarneChiRiho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/LindisfarneChiRiho.jpg/600px-LindisfarneChiRiho.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2905" data-file-height="4056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LindisfarneChiRiho.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page with <a href="/wiki/Chi_Rho" title="Chi Rho">Chi Rho</a> monogram from the Gospel of Matthew in the <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a> c. 700, possibly created by <a href="/wiki/Eadfrith_of_Lindisfarne" title="Eadfrith of Lindisfarne">Eadfrith of Lindisfarne</a> in memory of <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert" title="Cuthbert">Cuthbert</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Anglo-Saxons</b> were a cultural group who inhabited <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> from the <a href="/wiki/5th_century" title="5th century">5th century</a>. They comprise people from <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic tribes</a> who migrated to the island from <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a>, their descendants, and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">indigenous British groups</a> who adopted many aspects of Anglo-Saxon culture and language; the cultural foundations laid by the Anglo-Saxons are the foundation of the modern English legal system and of many aspects of English society; the modern English language owes over half its words – including the most common words of everyday speech – to the language of the Anglo-Saxons. Historically, the Anglo-Saxon period denotes the period in Britain between about 450 and 1066, after <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">their initial settlement</a> and up until the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Higham,_Nicholas_J._2013_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higham,_Nicholas_J._2013-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
The early Anglo-Saxon period includes the creation of an <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">English nation</a>, with many of the aspects that survive today, including regional government of <a href="/wiki/Shire" title="Shire">shires</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hundred_(county_division)" title="Hundred (county division)">hundreds</a>. During this period, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was established and there was a flowering of literature and language. Charters and law were also established.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>Anglo-Saxon</i> is popularly used for the language that was spoken and written by the Anglo-Saxons in England and eastern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> between at least the mid-5th century and the mid-12th century. In scholarly use, it is more commonly called <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The history of the Anglo-Saxons is the history of a cultural identity. It developed from divergent groups in association with the people's adoption of Christianity, and was integral to the establishment of various kingdoms. Threatened by extended <a href="/wiki/Danes" title="Danes">Danish</a> <a href="/wiki/Invasion" title="Invasion">invasions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_occupation" title="Military occupation">military occupation</a> of eastern England, this identity was re-established; it dominated until after the Norman Conquest.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The visible <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons#Culture" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon culture</a> can be seen in the material culture of <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_architecture" title="Anglo-Saxon architecture">buildings</a>, dress styles, <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated texts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grave_goods" title="Grave goods">grave goods</a>. Behind the symbolic nature of these cultural emblems, there are strong elements of tribal and lordship ties. The elite declared themselves as kings who developed <i><a href="/wiki/Burh" title="Burh">burhs</a></i>, and identified their roles and peoples in Biblical terms. Above all, as <a href="/wiki/Helena_Hamerow" title="Helena Hamerow">Helena Hamerow</a> has observed, "local and extended kin groups remained...the essential unit of production throughout the Anglo-Saxon period."<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The effects persist in the 21st century as, according to a study published in March 2015, the genetic makeup of British populations today shows divisions of the tribal political units of the early Anglo-Saxon period.<sup id="cite_ref-sarah_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sarah-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Use of the term <i>Anglo-Saxon</i> assumes that the words <i>Angles</i>, <i>Saxons</i> or <i>Anglo-Saxon</i> have the same meaning in all the sources. This term began to be used only in the 8th century to distinguish "Germanic" groups in Britain from those on the continent (<a href="/wiki/Old_Saxony" title="Old Saxony">Old Saxony</a> and 
from the <a href="/wiki/Anglia_(peninsula)" title="Anglia (peninsula)">Anglia region</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Germany" title="Northern Germany">Northern Germany</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> Catherine Hills summarised the views of many modern scholars in her observation that attitudes towards Anglo-Saxons, and hence the interpretation of their culture and history, have been "more contingent on contemporary political and religious theology as on any kind of evidence."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnonym">Ethnonym</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">ethnonym</a> <i>"Angul-Seaxan"</i> comes from the Latin <i>Angli-Saxones</i> and became the name of the peoples <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> calls <i>Angli</i><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Gildas" title="Gildas">Gildas</a> calls <i>Saxones</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Anglo-Saxon is a term that was rarely used by Anglo-Saxons themselves; it is not an <a href="/wiki/Exonym_and_endonym" title="Exonym and endonym">autonym</a>. It is likely they identified as <i>ængli</i>, <i>Seaxe</i> or, more probably, a local or tribal name such as <i>Mierce</i>, <i>Cantie</i>, <i>Gewisse</i>, <i>Westseaxe</i>, or <i>Norþanhymbre</i>. Also, the use of Anglo-Saxon disguises the extent to which people identified as <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Anglo-Scandinavian</a> after the Viking age, or as <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Anglo-Norman</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest</a> in 1066.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest historical references using this term are from outside Britain, referring to piratical Germanic raiders, 'Saxones' who attacked the shores of Britain and <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> in the 3rd century AD. <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a> states that Britain was settled by three races: the Angiloi, Frisones, and Britons.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>Angli Saxones</i> seems to have first been used in continental writing of the 8th century; <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Deacon" title="Paul the Deacon">Paul the Deacon</a> uses it to distinguish the English Saxons from the continental Saxons (<i>Ealdseaxe</i>, literally, 'old Saxons').<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The name therefore seemed to mean "English" Saxons.
</p><p>The Christian church seems to have used the word Angli; for example in the story of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Pope Gregory I</a> and his remark, "<i>Non Angli sed angeli</i>" (not English but angels).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The terms <i>ænglisc</i> ('the language') and <i>Angelcynn</i> ('the people') were also used by West Saxon <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">King Alfred</a> to refer to the people; in doing so he was following established practice.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The first use of the term Anglo-Saxon amongst the insular sources is in the titles for Athelstan: <i>Angelsaxonum Denorumque gloriosissimus rex</i> (most glorious king of the Anglo-Saxons and of the Danes) and <i>rex Angulsexna and Norþhymbra imperator paganorum gubernator Brittanorumque propugnator</i> (king of the Anglo-Saxons and emperor of the Northumbrians, governor of the pagans, and defender of the Britons). At other times he uses the term <i>rex Anglorum</i> (king of the English), which presumably meant both Anglo-Saxons and Danes. <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a> used <i>Anglosaxonum Rex</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>Engla cyningc</i> (King of the English) is used by Æthelred. King <a href="/wiki/Cnut_the_Great" title="Cnut the Great">Cnut</a> in 1021 was the first to refer to the land and not the people with this term: <i>ealles Englalandes cyningc</i> (King of all England).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> These titles express the sense that the Anglo-Saxons were a Christian people with a king anointed by God.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The indigenous <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Common Brittonic</a> speakers referred to Anglo-Saxons as <i>Saxones</i> or possibly <i>Saeson</i> (the word <i>Saeson</i> is the modern Welsh word for 'English people'); the equivalent word in <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/Sassenach" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassenach">Sasannach</a></i> and in the <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish language</a>, <i>Sasanach</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Catherine Hills suggests that it is no accident, "that the English call themselves by the name sanctified by the Church, as that of a people chosen by God, whereas their enemies use the name originally applied to piratical raiders".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_meanings">Contemporary meanings</span></h2>
<p>The Anglo-Saxons are the direct ancestors of the majority of the modern British people.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><i>Anglo-Saxon</i> in linguistics is still used as a term for the original <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> component of the modern <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>, which was later expanded and developed through the influence of <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Norman French</a>, though linguists now more often refer to it as <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>.
</p><p>Throughout the history of the Anglo-Saxons studies producing a dispassionate narrative of the people has been difficult. In the early Middle Ages the views of <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a> produced a personally inspired history that wasn't challenged for five hundred years. In the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a>, churchmen looking for signs of an English church reinterpreted Anglo-Saxon Christianity. In the 19th century the term <i>Anglo-Saxon</i> was broadly used in <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a>, and is sometimes so used at present. In Victorian Britain, some writers such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Knox" title="Robert Knox">Robert Knox</a>,
<a href="/wiki/James_Anthony_Froude" title="James Anthony Froude">James Anthony Froude</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kingsley" title="Charles Kingsley">Charles Kingsley</a><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_A._Freeman" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward A. Freeman">Edward A. Freeman</a><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> used the term <i>Anglo-Saxon</i> to justify <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a>, claiming that the "Anglo-Saxon" ancestry of the English made them racially superior to the colonised peoples. Similar racist ideas were advocated in the 19th-century United States by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_George_Morton" title="Samuel George Morton">Samuel George Morton</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Fitzhugh" title="George Fitzhugh">George Fitzhugh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> These views have influenced how versions of early English history are embedded in the sub-conscious of people "re-emerging in school textbooks and television programmes and still very congenial to some strands of political thinking."<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>Anglo-Saxon</i> is sometimes used to refer to peoples descended or associated in some way with the <a href="/wiki/English_(ethnic_group)" class="mw-redirect" title="English (ethnic group)">English ethnic group</a>, but there is no universal definition for the term. In contemporary <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">Anglophone</a> cultures outside Britain, "Anglo-Saxon" may be contrasted with "Celtic" as a socioeconomic identifier, invoking or reinforcing historical prejudices against non-English British immigrants. "<a href="/wiki/White_Anglo-Saxon_Protestant" title="White Anglo-Saxon Protestant">White Anglo-Saxon Protestant</a>", i.e. <a href="/wiki/White_Anglo-Saxon_Protestant" title="White Anglo-Saxon Protestant">WASP</a>, is a term especially popular in the United States that refers chiefly to old wealthy families with mostly English ancestors. As such, WASP is not a historical label or a precise ethnological term, but rather a (often derogatory) reference to contemporary family-based political, financial and cultural power— e.g., <a href="/wiki/Boston_Brahmin" title="Boston Brahmin">The Boston Brahmin</a>.
</p><p>Outside Anglophone countries in Europe and in the rest of the world, the term <i>Anglo-Saxon</i> and its direct translations are used to refer to the Anglophone peoples and societies of Britain, the United States, and other countries such as Australia, Canada and New Zealand–areas which are sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Anglosphere" title="Anglosphere">Anglosphere</a>. The term <i>Anglo-Saxon</i> can be used in a variety of contexts, often to identify the English-speaking world's distinctive language, culture, technology, wealth, markets, economy, and legal systems. Variations include the <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> "Angelsachsen", <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> "Anglo-Saxon", <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> "anglosajón", <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> "Anglo-saxão", <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> "англосаксы", <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> "anglosaksoński", <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> "anglosassone", <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a> "anglosaxó" and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> "Angurosakuson". As with the English-language use of the term, what constitutes the "Anglo-Saxon" varies from speaker to speaker.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In modern parlance, Anglo-Saxon is increasingly used to define a set of economic belief and systems rooted in anglophone countries. Characteristics of this model include low levels of regulation and taxes, and the public sector providing very few services. It can also mean strong private property rights, contract enforcement, and overall ease of doing business as well as low barriers to free trade. The Anglo-Saxon model is seen distinctly and competes versus other recognized economic models like continental bureaucracy or Chinese socialist model of central control.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Wikipedia_and_sources_that_mirror_or_use_it" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim cites another Wikipedia article. Articles need references to reliable third-party sources. (June 2018)">circular reference</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Early_Anglo-Saxon_history_.28410.E2.80.93660.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Anglo-Saxon_history_(410–660)">Early Anglo-Saxon history (410–660)</span></h2>
<p>The early Anglo-Saxon period covers the history of <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> Britain that starts from the end of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman rule</a>. It is a period widely known in European history as the <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a>, also the <i>Völkerwanderung</i><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> ("migration of peoples" in <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>). This was a period of intensified <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">human migration</a> in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> from about 375 to 800.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> The migrants were <a href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic tribes</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Suebi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frisii" title="Frisii">Frisii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>; they were later pushed westwards by the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avars_(Carpathians)" class="mw-redirect" title="Avars (Carpathians)">Avars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The migrants to Britain might also have included the Huns and <a href="/wiki/Rugini" title="Rugini">Rugini</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>123–124</span></sup>
</p><p>By the year 400, southern Britain – that is Britain south of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> – was a peripheral part of the western Roman Empire, occasionally lost to rebellion or invasion, but until then always eventually recovered. Around 410, Britain slipped beyond direct imperial control into a phase which has generally been termed "<a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Sub-Roman">sub-Roman</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Migration_.28410.E2.80.93560.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_(410–560)">Migration (410–560)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo.Saxon.migration.5th.cen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Anglo.Saxon.migration.5th.cen.jpg/280px-Anglo.Saxon.migration.5th.cen.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Anglo.Saxon.migration.5th.cen.jpg/420px-Anglo.Saxon.migration.5th.cen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Anglo.Saxon.migration.5th.cen.jpg/560px-Anglo.Saxon.migration.5th.cen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1703" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo.Saxon.migration.5th.cen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The migrations according to Bede, who wrote some 300 years after the event; there is archeological evidence that the settlers in England came from many of these continental locations</div></div></div>
<p>The traditional narrative of this period is one of decline and fall, invasion and migration; however, the archaeologist<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Heinrich Härke stated in 2011:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is now widely accepted that the Anglo-Saxons were not just transplanted Germanic invaders and settlers from the Continent, but the outcome of insular interactions and changes.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Writing c. 540 <a href="/wiki/Gildas" title="Gildas">Gildas</a> mentions that, sometime in the 5th century, a council of leaders in Britain agreed that some land in the east of southern Britain would be given to the Saxons on the basis of a treaty, a <i>foedus,</i> by which the Saxons would defend the Britons against attacks from the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scoti" title="Scoti">Scoti</a> in exchange for food supplies. The most contemporaneous textual evidence is the <a href="/wiki/Chronica_Gallica_of_452" title="Chronica Gallica of 452">Chronica Gallica of 452</a> which records for the year 441: "The British provinces, which to this time had suffered various defeats and misfortunes, are reduced to Saxon rule."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This is an earlier date than that of 451 for the "coming of the Saxons" used by <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_ecclesiastica_gentis_Anglorum" class="mw-redirect" title="Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum">Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum</a>,</i> written around 731. It has been argued that Bede misinterpreted his (scanty) sources, and that the chronological references in the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Britonnum" class="mw-redirect" title="Historia Britonnum">Historia Britonnum</a></i> yield a plausible date of around 428.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gildas" title="Gildas">Gildas</a> recounts how a war broke out between the Saxons and the local population – Higham calls it the "War of the Saxon Federates" – which ended shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Badon" title="Battle of Badon">siege at 'Mons Badonicus'</a>. The Saxons go back to "their eastern home". Gildas calls the peace a "grievous divorce with the barbarians". The price of peace, Nick Higham argues,<sup id="cite_ref-Higham,_Nick_2004_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higham,_Nick_2004-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> is a better treaty for the Saxons, giving them the ability to receive tribute from people across the lowlands of Britain. The archaeological evidence agrees with this earlier timescale. In particular, the work of Catherine Hills and Sam Lucy on the evidence of <a href="/wiki/Spong_Hill" title="Spong Hill">Spong Hill</a> has moved the chronology for the settlement earlier than 450, with a significant number of items now in phases before Bede's date.<sup id="cite_ref-Hills._C,_&amp;_Lucy,_S._39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hills._C,_&amp;_Lucy,_S.-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This vision of the Anglo-Saxons exercising extensive political and military power at an early date remains contested. The most developed vision of a continuation in sub-Roman Britain, with control over its own political and military destiny for well over a century, is that of Kenneth Dark,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> who suggests that the sub-Roman elite survived in culture, politics and military power up to c. 570. However, Nick Higham seems to agree with <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>, who identified three phases of settlement: an exploration phase, when mercenaries came to protect the resident population; a migration phase, which was substantial as implied by the statement that <i>Anglus</i> was deserted; and an establishment phase, in which Anglo-Saxons started to control areas, implied in Bede's statement about the origins of the tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars have not reached consensus on the number of migrants who entered Britain in this period. Heinrich Härke suggests that the figure is around 100,000,<sup id="cite_ref-Härke,_Heinrich_2011_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Härke,_Heinrich_2011-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> based on the molecular evidence. But, archaeologists such as Christine Hills<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and Richard Hodges<sup id="cite_ref-Hedges,_Robert_2011._pp_81-83_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hedges,_Robert_2011._pp_81-83-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> suggest the number is nearer 20,000. By around 500 the Anglo-Saxon migrants were established in southern and eastern Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>What happened to the indigenous Brittonic people is also subject to question. Heinrich Härke and Richard Coates<sup id="cite_ref-Coates,_Richard_2004_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coates,_Richard_2004-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> point out that they are invisible archaeologically and linguistically. But based on a fairly high Anglo-Saxon figure (200,000) and a low Brythonic one (800,000), Brythonic people are likely to have outnumbered Anglo-Saxons by at least four to one. The interpretation of such figures is that while "culturally, the later Anglo-Saxons and English did emerge as remarkably un-British, . . . their genetic, biological make-up is none the less likely to have been substantially, indeed predominantly, British".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The development of Anglo-Saxon culture is described by two processes. One is similar to culture changes observed in Russia, North Africa and parts of the Islamic world, where a powerful minority culture becomes, over a rather short period, adopted by a settled majority.<sup id="cite_ref-Ward-Perkins,_Bryan_2000_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-Perkins,_Bryan_2000-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The second process is explained through incentives. Nick Higham summarized in this way:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As Bede later implied, language was a key indicator of ethnicity in early England. In circumstances where freedom at law, acceptance with the kindred, access to patronage, and the use and possession of weapons were all exclusive to those who could claim Germanic descent, then speaking Old English without Latin or Brittonic inflection had considerable value.<sup id="cite_ref-Higham,_Nicholas_J._2013_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higham,_Nicholas_J._2013-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tribal_Hidage_Spelman.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/Tribal_Hidage_Spelman.svg/240px-Tribal_Hidage_Spelman.svg.png" decoding="async" width="240" height="590" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/Tribal_Hidage_Spelman.svg/360px-Tribal_Hidage_Spelman.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/Tribal_Hidage_Spelman.svg/480px-Tribal_Hidage_Spelman.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="705" data-file-height="1733" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tribal_Hidage_Spelman.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Tribal_Hidage" title="Tribal Hidage">Tribal Hidage</a>, from an edition of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Spelman" title="Henry Spelman">Henry Spelman</a>'s <i>Glossarium Archaiologicum</i></div></div></div>
<p>By the middle of the 6th century, some Brythonic people in the lowlands of Britain had moved across the sea to form <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, and some had moved west, but the majority were abandoning their past language and culture and adopting the new culture of the Anglo-Saxons. As they adopted this language and culture, the barriers began to dissolve between peoples, who had earlier lived parallel lives.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas,_Mark_G._2006_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas,_Mark_G._2006-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The archaeological evidence shows considerable continuity in the system of landscape and local governance,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> which was inherited from the indigenous community. There is evidence for a fusion of culture in this early period.<sup id="cite_ref-Suzuki,_Seiichi_2000_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suzuki,_Seiichi_2000-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Brythonic names appear in the lists of Anglo-Saxon elite. The <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> royal line was traditionally founded by a man named <a href="/wiki/Cerdic_of_Wessex" title="Cerdic of Wessex">Cerdic</a>, an undoubtedly Celtic name ultimately derived from <a href="/wiki/Caratacus" title="Caratacus">Caratacus</a>. This may indicate that Cerdic was a native Briton, and that his dynasty became anglicised over time.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Myres,_J.N.L._1989_pp._146_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myres,_J.N.L._1989_pp._146-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> A number of Cerdic's alleged descendants also possessed Celtic names, including the '<a href="/wiki/Bretwalda" title="Bretwalda">Bretwalda</a>' <a href="/wiki/Ceawlin_of_Wessex" title="Ceawlin of Wessex">Ceawlin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The last man in this dynasty to have a Brythonic name was King <a href="/wiki/Caedwalla" class="mw-redirect" title="Caedwalla">Caedwalla</a>, who died as late as 689.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Development_of_an_Anglo-Saxon_society_.28560.E2.80.93610.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_an_Anglo-Saxon_society_(560–610)">Development of an Anglo-Saxon society (560–610)</span></h3>
<p>In the last half of the 6th century, four structures contributed to the development of society; they were the position and freedoms of the <i>ceorl,</i> the smaller tribal areas coalescing into larger kingdoms, the elite developing from warriors to kings, and Irish monasticism developing under <a href="/wiki/Finnian_of_Movilla" title="Finnian of Movilla">Finnian</a> (who had consulted <a href="/wiki/Gildas" title="Gildas">Gildas</a>) and his pupil <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a>.
</p><p>The Anglo-Saxon farms of this period are often falsely supposed to be "peasant farms". However, a <a href="/wiki/Churl" title="Churl"><i>ceorl</i></a>, who was the lowest ranking freeman in early Anglo-Saxon society, was not a peasant but an arms-owning male with the support of a kindred, access to law and the <i><a href="/wiki/Wergild" class="mw-redirect" title="Wergild">wergild</a></i>; situated at the apex of an extended household working at least one hide of land.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The farmer had freedom and rights over lands, with provision of a rent or duty to an overlord who provided only slight lordly input.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> Most of this land was common outfield arable land (of an outfield-infield system) that provided individuals with the means to build a basis of kinship and group cultural ties.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tribal_Hidage" title="Tribal Hidage">Tribal Hidage</a> lists thirty-five peoples, or tribes, with assessments in hides, which may have originally been defined as the area of land sufficient to maintain one family.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The assessments in the <i>Hidage</i> reflect the relative size of the provinces.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke,_Barbara_2002_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke,_Barbara_2002-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Although varying in size, all thirty-five peoples of the Tribal Hidage were of the same status, in that they were areas which were ruled by their own elite family (or royal houses), and so were assessed independently for payment of tribute. <sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the sixth century, larger kingdoms had become established on the south or east coasts.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> They include the provinces of the <a href="/wiki/Jutes#Jutish_settlement_in_Southern_Britain" title="Jutes">Jutes of Hampshire and Wight</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sussex" title="Kingdom of Sussex">South Saxons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kent" title="Kingdom of Kent">Kent</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Essex" title="Kingdom of Essex">East Saxons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_East_Anglia" title="Kingdom of East Anglia">East Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lindsey" title="Kingdom of Lindsey">Lindsey</a> and (north of the Humber) <a href="/wiki/Deira" title="Deira">Deira</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a>. Several of these kingdoms may have had as their initial focus a territory based on a former Roman <i><a href="/wiki/Civitas" title="Civitas">civitas</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the sixth century, the leaders of these communities were styling themselves kings, though it should not be assumed that all of them were Germanic in origin. The <a href="/wiki/Bretwalda" title="Bretwalda">Bretwalda</a> concept is taken as evidence of a number of early Anglo-Saxon elite families. What Bede seems to imply in his <i>Bretwalda</i> is the ability of leaders to extract tribute, overawe and/or protect the small regions, which may well have been relatively short-lived in any one instance. Ostensibly "Anglo-Saxon" dynasties variously replaced one another in this role in a discontinuous but influential and potent roll call of warrior elites.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Importantly, whatever their origin or whenever they flourished, these dynasties established their claim to lordship through their links to extended kin ties. As Helen Peake jokingly points out, "they all just happened to be related back to Woden".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The process from warrior to <i>cyning</i> – Old English for king – is described in <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i>:
</p>
<table cellpadding="6" align="left">

<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:50%;"><i><b>Old English</b></i>
</td>
<td style="width:50%;"><i><b>Modern English (as translated by <a href="/wiki/Seamus_Heaney" title="Seamus Heaney">Seamus Heaney</a>)</b></i>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align:top; white-space:nowrap;">
<td>
<p><i>Oft Scyld Scéfing – sceaþena þréatum</i><br />
<i>monegum maégþum – meodosetla oftéah•</i><br />
<i>egsode Eorle – syððan aérest wearð</i><br />
<i>féasceaft funde – hé þæs frófre gebád•</i><br />
<i>wéox under wolcnum – weorðmyndum þáh</i><br />
<i>oð þæt him aéghwylc – þára ymbsittendra</i><br />
<i>ofer hronráde – hýran scolde,</i><br />
<i>gomban gyldan – þæt wæs gód cyning.</i><br />
</p>
</td>
<td>
<p>There was Shield Sheafson, scourge of many tribes,<br />
A wrecker of mead-benches, rampaging among foes.<br />
This terror of the hall-troops had come far.<br />
A foundling to start with, he would flourish later on<br />
As his powers waxed and his worth was proved.<br />
In the end each clan on the outlying coasts<br />
Beyond the whale-road had to yield to him<br />
And begin to pay tribute. That was one good king.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span id="Conversion_to_Christianity_.28590.E2.80.93660.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_to_Christianity_(590–660)">Conversion to Christianity (590–660)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Athelstan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Athelstan.jpg/240px-Athelstan.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Athelstan.jpg/360px-Athelstan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Athelstan.jpg/480px-Athelstan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1347" data-file-height="1898" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Athelstan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Æthelstan presenting a <a href="/wiki/Gospel_book" class="mw-redirect" title="Gospel book">gospel book</a> to (the long-dead) St <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert" title="Cuthbert">Cuthbert</a> (934); Corpus Christi College Cambridge MS 183, fol. 1v</div></div></div>
<p>In 565, <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a>, a monk from Ireland who studied at the monastic school of <a href="/wiki/Moville" title="Moville">Moville</a> under St. <a href="/wiki/Finnian_of_Movilla" title="Finnian of Movilla">Finnian</a>, reached <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> as a self-imposed exile. The influence of the monastery of Iona would grow into what <a href="/wiki/Peter_Brown_(historian)" title="Peter Brown (historian)">Peter Brown</a> has described as an "unusually extensive spiritual empire," which "stretched from western Scotland deep to the southwest into the heart of Ireland and, to the southeast, it reached down throughout northern Britain, through the influence of its sister monastery Lindisfarne."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 597 Columba died. At this time, <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine</a> landed on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Thanet" title="Isle of Thanet">Isle of Thanet</a> and proceeded to King <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelberht_of_Kent" title="Æthelberht of Kent">Æthelberht</a>'s main town of <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">Canterbury</a>. He had been the <a href="/wiki/Prior" title="Prior">prior</a> of a monastery in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> when Pope <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Gregory the Great</a> chose him in 595 to lead the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_mission" title="Gregorian mission">Gregorian mission</a> to Britain to <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianise</a> the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kent" title="Kingdom of Kent">Kingdom of Kent</a> from their native <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_paganism" title="Anglo-Saxon paganism">Anglo-Saxon paganism</a>. Kent was probably chosen because Æthelberht had married a Christian princess, <a href="/wiki/Bertha_of_Kent" title="Bertha of Kent">Bertha</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Charibert_I" title="Charibert I">Charibert I</a> the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Frankish_kings" title="List of Frankish kings">King of Paris</a>, who was expected to exert some influence over her husband. Æthelberht was converted to Christianity, churches were established, and wider-scale conversion to Christianity began in the kingdom. <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelberht%27s_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Æthelberht&#39;s law">Æthelberht's law</a> for Kent, the earliest written code in any <a href="/wiki/Germanic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic language">Germanic language</a>, instituted a complex system of fines. Kent was rich, with strong trade ties to the continent, and Æthelberht may have instituted royal control over trade. For the first time following the Anglo-Saxon invasion, coins began circulating in Kent during his reign.
</p><p>In 635 <a href="/wiki/Aidan_of_Lindisfarne" title="Aidan of Lindisfarne">Aidan</a>, an Irish monk from Iona, chose the <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Isle of Lindisfarne</a> to establish a monastery which was close to King <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald</a>'s main fortress of <a href="/wiki/Bamburgh" title="Bamburgh">Bamburgh</a>. He had been at the monastery in <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> when Oswald asked to be sent a mission to <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianise</a> the <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Kingdom of Northumbria</a> from their native <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_paganism" title="Anglo-Saxon paganism">Anglo-Saxon paganism</a>. Oswald had probably chosen <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> because after his father had been killed he had fled into south-west Scotland and had encountered Christianity, and had returned determined to make Northumbria Christian. Aidan achieved great success in spreading the Christian faith, and since Aidan could not speak English and Oswald had learned Irish during his exile, Oswald acted as Aidan's interpreter when the latter was preaching.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Later, <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>'s patron saint, <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert_of_Lindisfarne" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuthbert of Lindisfarne">Saint Cuthbert</a>, was an <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbot</a> of the monastery, and then <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Lindisfarne" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Lindisfarne">Bishop of Lindisfarne</a>. An anonymous life of Cuthbert written at Lindisfarne is the oldest extant piece of English historical writing, <sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> and in his memory a gospel (known as the <a href="/wiki/St_Cuthbert_Gospel" title="St Cuthbert Gospel">St Cuthbert Gospel</a>) was placed in his coffin. The decorated leather <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding" title="Bookbinding">bookbinding</a> is the oldest intact European binding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198280–81_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198280–81-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 664, the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Whitby" title="Synod of Whitby">Synod of Whitby</a> was convened and established Roman practice as opposed to Irish practice (in style of tonsure and dates of Easter) as the norm in Northumbria, and thus "brought the <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbrian</a> church into the mainstream of Roman culture."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The episcopal seat of Northumbria was transferred from <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a> to <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid" title="Wilfrid">Wilfrid</a>, chief advocate for the Roman position, later became Bishop of Northumbria, while <a href="/wiki/Colm%C3%A1n" class="mw-redirect" title="Colmán">Colmán</a> and the Ionan supporters, who did not change their practices, withdrew to Iona.
</p>
<h2><span id="Middle_Anglo-Saxon_history_.28660.E2.80.93899.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Anglo-Saxon_history_(660–899)">Middle Anglo-Saxon history (660–899)</span></h2>
<p>By 660 the political map of <a href="/wiki/Tees-Exe_line" title="Tees-Exe line">Lowland Britain</a> had developed with smaller territories coalescing into kingdoms, and from this time larger kingdoms started dominating the smaller kingdoms. The development of kingdoms, with a particular king being recognised as an overlord, developed out of an early loose structure that, Higham believes, is linked back to the original <i>feodus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The traditional name for this period is the <a href="/wiki/Heptarchy" title="Heptarchy">Heptarchy</a>, which has not been used by scholars since the early 20th century<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke,_Barbara_2002_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke,_Barbara_2002-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> as it gives the impression of a single political structure and does not afford the "opportunity to treat the history of any one kingdom as a whole".<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes,_Simon_1995_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes,_Simon_1995-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Simon Keynes suggests that the 8th and 9th century was period of economic and social flourishing which created stability both below the Thames and above the <a href="/wiki/Humber" title="Humber">Humber</a>. Many areas flourished and their influence was felt across the continent, however in between the Humber and Thames, one political entity grew in influence and power and to the East these developments in Britain attracted attention.<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes,_Simon_1995_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes,_Simon_1995-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mercian_supremacy_.28626.E2.80.93821.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercian_supremacy_(626–821)">Mercian supremacy (626–821)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mercian_Supremacy" title="Mercian Supremacy">Mercian Supremacy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:England_green_top.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/England_green_top.svg/280px-England_green_top.svg.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="352" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/England_green_top.svg/420px-England_green_top.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/England_green_top.svg/560px-England_green_top.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="963" data-file-height="1210" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:England_green_top.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A political map of Britain circa 650 (the names are in modern English)</div></div></div>
<p>Middle-lowland Britain was known as the place of the <i>Mierce</i>, the border or frontier folk, in Latin Mercia. Mercia was a diverse area of tribal groups, as shown by the <a href="/wiki/Tribal_Hidage" title="Tribal Hidage">Tribal Hidage</a>; the peoples were a mixture of Brythonic speaking peoples and "Anglo-Saxon" pioneers and their early leaders had Brythonic names, such as <a href="/wiki/Penda_of_Mercia" title="Penda of Mercia">Penda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke,_Barbara_p101_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke,_Barbara_p101-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Although Penda does not appear in Bede's list of great overlords it would appear from what Bede says elsewhere that he was dominant over the southern kingdoms. At the time of the battle of the river Winwæd, thirty <i>duces regii</i> (royal generals) fought on his behalf. Although there are many gaps in the evidence, it is clear that the seventh-century Mercian kings were formidable rulers who were able to exercise a wide-ranging overlordship from their <a href="/wiki/The_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="The Midlands">Midland</a> base.
</p><p>Mercian military success was the basis of their power; it succeeded against not only 106 kings and kingdoms by winning set-piece battles,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> but by ruthlessly ravaging any area foolish enough to withhold tribute. There are a number of casual references scattered throughout the <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>'s history to this aspect of Mercian military policy. Penda is found ravaging Northumbria as far north as <a href="/wiki/Bamburgh" title="Bamburgh">Bamburgh</a> and only a miraculous intervention from Aidan prevents the complete destruction of the settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In 676 <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred_of_Mercia" title="Æthelred of Mercia">Æthelred</a> conducted a similar ravaging in Kent and caused such damage in the <a href="/wiki/Rochester,_Kent" title="Rochester, Kent">Rochester</a> diocese that two successive bishops gave up their position because of lack of funds.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke,_Barbara_p101_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke,_Barbara_p101-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In these accounts there is a rare glimpse of the realities of early Anglo-Saxon overlordship and how a widespread overlordship could be established in a relatively short period. By the middle of the 8th century, other kingdoms of southern Britain were also affected by Mercian expansionism. The East Saxons seem to have lost control of London, <a href="/wiki/Middlesex" title="Middlesex">Middlesex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">Hertfordshire</a> to Æthelbald, although the East Saxon homelands do not seem to have been affected, and the East Saxon dynasty continued into the ninth century.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The Mercian influence and reputation reached its peak when, in the late 8th century, the most powerful European ruler of the age, the Frankish king <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, recognised <a href="/wiki/Offa_of_Mercia" title="Offa of Mercia">the Mercian King Offa</a>'s power and accordingly treated him with respect, even if this could have been just flattery.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Learning_and_monasticism_.28660.E2.80.93793.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Learning_and_monasticism_(660–793)">Learning and monasticism (660–793)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Britain_802.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Britain_802.jpg/220px-Britain_802.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Britain_802.jpg/330px-Britain_802.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Britain_802.jpg/440px-Britain_802.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1241" data-file-height="1755" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Britain_802.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Britain in 802</div></div></div>
<p>Michael Drout calls this period the "Golden Age", when learning flourishes with a renaissance in classical knowledge. The growth and popularity of monasticism was not an entirely internal development, with influence from the continent shaping Anglo-Saxon monastic life.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In 669 <a href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Tarsus" title="Theodore of Tarsus">Theodore</a>, a Greek-speaking monk originally from Tarsus in Asia Minor, arrived in Britain to become the eighth <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>. He was joined the following year by his colleague Hadrian, a Latin-speaking African by origin and former abbot of a monastery in Campania (near Naples).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> One of their first tasks at Canterbury was the establishment of a school; and according to <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> (writing some sixty years later), they soon "attracted a crowd of students into whose minds they daily poured the streams of wholesome learning".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> As evidence of their teaching, Bede reports that some of their students, who survived to his own day were as fluent in Greek and Latin as in their native language. Bede does not mention <a href="/wiki/Aldhelm" title="Aldhelm">Aldhelm</a> in this connection; but we know from a letter addressed by Aldhelm to Hadrian that he too must be numbered among their students.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aldhelm wrote in elaborate and grandiloquent and very difficult Latin, which became the dominant style for centuries. Michael Drout states "Aldhelm wrote Latin hexameters better than anyone before in England (and possibly better than anyone since, or at least up until Milton). His work showed that scholars in England, at the very edge of Europe, could be as learned and sophisticated as any writers in Europe."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> During this period, the wealth and power of the monasteries increased as elite families, possibly out of power, turned to monastic life.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Anglo-Saxon monasticism developed the unusual institution of the "double monastery", a house of monks and a house of nuns, living next to each other, sharing a church but never mixing, and living separate lives of celibacy. These double monasteries were presided over by abbesses, some of the most powerful and influential women in Europe. Double monasteries which were built on strategic sites near rivers and coasts, accumulated immense wealth and power over multiple generations (their inheritances were not divided) and became centers of art and learning.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Aldhelm was doing his work in <a href="/wiki/Malmesbury" title="Malmesbury">Malmesbury</a>, far from him, up in the North of England, <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> was writing a large quantity of books, gaining a reputation in Europe and showing that the English could write history and theology, and do astronomical computation (for the dates of Easter, among other things).
</p>
<h3><span id="West_Saxon_hegemony_and_the_Anglo-Scandinavian_Wars_.28793.E2.80.93878.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Saxon_hegemony_and_the_Anglo-Scandinavian_Wars_(793–878)">West Saxon hegemony and the Anglo-Scandinavian Wars (793–878)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exhibition_in_Viking_Ship_Museum,_Oslo_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Exhibition_in_Viking_Ship_Museum%2C_Oslo_01.jpg/260px-Exhibition_in_Viking_Ship_Museum%2C_Oslo_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="347" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Exhibition_in_Viking_Ship_Museum%2C_Oslo_01.jpg/390px-Exhibition_in_Viking_Ship_Museum%2C_Oslo_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Exhibition_in_Viking_Ship_Museum%2C_Oslo_01.jpg/520px-Exhibition_in_Viking_Ship_Museum%2C_Oslo_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exhibition_in_Viking_Ship_Museum,_Oslo_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Oseberg_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Oseberg ship">Oseberg ship</a> prow, <a href="/wiki/Viking_Ship_Museum_(Oslo)" title="Viking Ship Museum (Oslo)">Viking Ship Museum</a>, Oslo, Norway.</div></div></div>
<p>The 9th century saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, from the foundations laid by <a href="/wiki/Egbert_of_Wessex" class="mw-redirect" title="Egbert of Wessex">King Egbert</a> in the first quarter of the century to the achievements of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">King Alfred the Great</a> in its closing decades. The outlines of the story are told in the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i>, though the annals represent a West Saxon point of view.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> On the day of Egbert's succession to the kingdom of Wessex, in 802, a Mercian ealdorman from the province of the <i><a href="/wiki/Hwicce" title="Hwicce">Hwicce</a></i> had crossed the border at <a href="/wiki/Kempsford" title="Kempsford">Kempsford</a>, with the intention of mounting a raid into northern Wiltshire; the Mercian force was met by the local ealdorman, "and the people of Wiltshire had the victory".<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In 829 Egbert went on, the chronicler reports, to conquer "the kingdom of the Mercians and everything south of the Humber".<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> It was at this point that the chronicler chose to attach Egbert's name to Bede's list of seven overlords, adding that "he was the eighth king who was Bretwalda".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Simon Keynes suggests Egbert's foundation of a 'bipartite' kingdom is crucial as it stretched across southern England, and it created a working alliance between the West Saxon dynasty and the rulers of the Mercians.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In 860 the eastern and western parts of the southern kingdom were united by agreement between the surviving sons of <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwulf_of_Wessex" class="mw-redirect" title="Æthelwulf of Wessex">King Æthelwulf</a>, though the union was not maintained without some opposition from within the dynasty; and in the late 870s King Alfred gained the submission of the Mercians under their ruler <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred,_Lord_of_the_Mercians" title="Æthelred, Lord of the Mercians">Æthelred</a>, who in other circumstances might have been styled a king, but who under the Alfredian regime was regarded as the 'ealdorman' of his people.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Viking_weight_combined_only_reflection.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Viking_weight_combined_only_reflection.jpg/220px-Viking_weight_combined_only_reflection.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Viking_weight_combined_only_reflection.jpg/330px-Viking_weight_combined_only_reflection.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Viking_weight_combined_only_reflection.jpg/440px-Viking_weight_combined_only_reflection.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1042" data-file-height="713" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Viking_weight_combined_only_reflection.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anglo-Saxon-Viking Coin weight. Material is lead and weighs approx 36&#160;g. Embedded with a sceat dating to 720–750 AD and minted in Kent. It is edged in dotted triangle pattern. Origin is the Danelaw region and dates late 8th to 9th century.</div></div></div>
<p>The wealth of the monasteries and the success of Anglo-Saxon society attracted the attention of people from continental Europe, mostly Danes and Norwegians. Due to the plundering raids that followed, the raiders attracted the name <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> – from the Old Norse <i>víkingr</i> meaning an expedition – which soon became used for the raiding activity or piracy reported in western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In 793, Lindisfarne was raided and while this was not the first raid of its type it was the most prominent. A year later Jarrow, the monastery where Bede wrote, was attacked; in 795 Iona; and in 804 the nunnery at Lyminge Kent was granted refuge inside the walls of Canterbury. Sometime around 800, a Reeve from Portland in Wessex was killed when he mistook some raiders for ordinary traders.
</p><p>Viking raids continued until in 850, then the <i>Chronicle</i> says: "The heathen for the first time remained over the winter". The fleet does not appear to have stayed long in England, but it started a trend which others subsequently followed. In particular, the army which arrived in 865 remained over many winters, and part of it later settled what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a>. This was the "<a href="/wiki/Great_Heathen_Army" title="Great Heathen Army">Great Army</a>", a term used by the <i>Chronicle</i> in England and by Adrevald of Fleury on the Continent. The invaders were able not only to exploit the feuds between and within the various kingdoms, but to appoint puppet kings, Ceolwulf in Mercia in 873, 'a foolish king's thane' (ASC), and perhaps others in Northumbria in 867 and East Anglia in 870.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The third phase was an era of settlement; however, the 'Great Army' went wherever it could find the richest pickings, crossing the Channel when faced with resolute opposition, as in England in 878, or with famine, as on the Continent in 892.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> By this stage the Vikings were assuming ever increasing importance as catalysts of social and political change. They constituted the common enemy, making the English the more conscious of a national identity which overrode deeper distinctions; they could be perceived as an instrument of divine punishment for the people's sins, raising awareness of a collective Christian identity; and by 'conquering' the kingdoms of the East Angles, the Northumbrians and the Mercians they created a vacuum in the leadership of the English people.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Danish settlement continued in Mercia in 877 and East Anglia in 879—80 and 896. The rest of the army meanwhile continued to harry and plunder on both sides of the Channel, with new recruits evidently arriving to swell its ranks, for it clearly continued to be a formidable fighting force.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965_90-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> At first, Alfred responded by the offer of repeated tribute payments. However, after a decisive victory at Edington in 878, Alfred offered vigorous opposition. He established a chain of fortresses across the south of England, reorganised the army, "so that always half its men were at home, and half out on service, except for those men who were to garrison the burhs" (A.SC s.a. 893),<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965_90-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and in 896 ordered a new type of craft to be built which could oppose the Viking longships in shallow coastal waters. When the Vikings returned from the Continent in 892, they found they could no longer roam the country at will, for wherever they went they were opposed by a local army. After four years, the Scandinavians therefore split up, some to settle in Northumbria and East Anglia, the remainder to try their luck again on the Continent.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965_90-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelock,_Dorothy_1965-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="King_Alfred_and_the_rebuilding_.28878.E2.80.93899.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="King_Alfred_and_the_rebuilding_(878–899)">King Alfred and the rebuilding (878–899)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfred_Jewel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Alfred_Jewel.jpg/240px-Alfred_Jewel.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Alfred_Jewel.jpg/360px-Alfred_Jewel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Alfred_Jewel.jpg/480px-Alfred_Jewel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1748" data-file-height="1879" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfred_Jewel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A royal gift, the <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Jewel" title="Alfred Jewel">Alfred Jewel</a></div></div></div>
<p>More important to Alfred than his military and political victories were his religion, his love of learning, and his spread of writing throughout England. Simon Keynes suggests Alfred's work laid the foundations for what really makes England unique in all of medieval Europe from around 800 until 1066.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> What is also unique is that we can discover some of this in Alfred's own words:
</p><p>Thinking about how learning and culture had fallen since the last century, he wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...So completely had wisdom fallen off in England that there were very few on this side of the Humber who could understand their rituals in English, or indeed could translate a letter from Latin into English; and I believe that there were not many beyond the Humber. There were so few of them that I indeed cannot think of a single one south of the Thames when I became king. (Preface: "Gregory the Great's Pastoral Care")<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes,_Simon_1984_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes,_Simon_1984-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Alfred knew that literature and learning, both in English and in Latin, were very important, but the state of learning was not good when Alfred came to the throne. Alfred saw kingship as a priestly office, a shepherd for his people.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> One book that was particularly valuable to him was Gregory the Great's <i>Cura Pastoralis</i> (Pastoral Care). This is a priest's guide on how to care for people. Alfred took this book as his own guide on how to be a good king to his people; hence, a good king to Alfred increases literacy. Alfred translated this book himself and explains in the preface:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...When I had learned it I translated it into English, just as I had understood it, and as I could most meaningfully render it. And I will send one to each bishopric in my kingdom, and in each will be an æstel worth fifty mancuses. And I command in God's name that no man may take the æstel from the book nor the book from the church. It is unknown how long there may be such learned bishops as, thanks to God, are nearly everywhere.(Preface: "Gregory the Great's Pastoral Care")<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes,_Simon_1984_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes,_Simon_1984-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>What is presumed to be one of these "æstel" (the word only appears in this one text) is the gold, <a href="/wiki/Rock_crystal" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock crystal">rock crystal</a> and enamel <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Jewel" title="Alfred Jewel">Alfred Jewel</a>, discovered in 1693, which is assumed to have been fitted with a small rod and used as a pointer when reading. Alfred provided functional patronage, linked to a social programme of vernacular literacy in England, which was unprecedented.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Therefore it seems better to me, if it seems so to you, that we also translate certain books ...and bring it about ...if we have the peace, that all the youth of free men who now are in England, those who have the means that they may apply themselves to it, be set to learning, while they may not be set to any other use, until the time when they can well read English writings. (Preface: "Gregory the Great's Pastoral Care")<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes,_Simon_1984_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes,_Simon_1984-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>This set in train a growth in charters, law, theology and learning. Alfred thus laid the foundation for the great accomplishments of the tenth century and did much to make the vernacular was more important than Latin in Anglo-Saxon culture.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I desired to live worthily as long as I lived, and to leave after my life, to the men who should come after me, the memory of me in good works. (Preface: "The Consolation of Philosophy by Boethius")<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes,_Simon_1984_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes,_Simon_1984-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h2><span id="Late_Anglo-Saxon_history_.28899.E2.80.931066.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Anglo-Saxon_history_(899–1066)">Late Anglo-Saxon history (899–1066)</span></h2>
<p>A framework for the momentous events of the 10th and 11th centuries is provided by the <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i>. However charters, law-codes and coins supply detailed information on various aspects of royal government, and the surviving works of Anglo-Latin and vernacular literature, as well as the numerous manuscripts written in the 10th century, testify in their different ways to the vitality of ecclesiastical culture. Yet as Simon Keynes suggests "it does not follow that the 10th century is better understood than more sparsely documented periods".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Reform_and_formation_of_England_.28899.E2.80.93978.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_and_formation_of_England_(899–978)">Reform and formation of England (899–978)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_the_Elder_coin_imitation_silver_brooch_Rome_Italy_c_920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Edward_the_Elder_coin_imitation_silver_brooch_Rome_Italy_c_920.jpg/240px-Edward_the_Elder_coin_imitation_silver_brooch_Rome_Italy_c_920.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Edward_the_Elder_coin_imitation_silver_brooch_Rome_Italy_c_920.jpg/360px-Edward_the_Elder_coin_imitation_silver_brooch_Rome_Italy_c_920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Edward_the_Elder_coin_imitation_silver_brooch_Rome_Italy_c_920.jpg/480px-Edward_the_Elder_coin_imitation_silver_brooch_Rome_Italy_c_920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1364" data-file-height="1368" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_the_Elder_coin_imitation_silver_brooch_Rome_Italy_c_920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver brooch imitating a coin of Edward the Elder, c. 920, found in Rome, Italy. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>During the course of the 10th century, the West Saxon kings extended their power first over <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>, then into the southern <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a>, and finally over <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>, thereby imposing a semblance of political unity on peoples, who nonetheless would remain conscious of their respective customs and their separate pasts. The prestige, and indeed the pretensions, of the monarchy increased, the institutions of government strengthened, and kings and their agents sought in various ways to establish social order.<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes,_Simon_2001_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes,_Simon_2001-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> This process started with <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Elder" title="Edward the Elder">Edward the Elder</a> – who with his sister, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfl%C3%A6d" title="Æthelflæd">Æthelflæd</a>, Lady of the Mercians, initially, charters reveal, encouraged people to purchase estates from the Danes, thereby to reassert some degree of English influence in territory which had fallen under Danish control. David Dumville suggests that Edward may have extended this policy by rewarding his supporters with grants of land in the territories newly conquered from the Danes, and that any charters issued in respect of such grants have not survived.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> When Athelflæd died, Mercia was absorbed by Wessex. From that point on there was no contest for the throne, so the house of Wessex became the ruling house of England.<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes,_Simon_2001_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes,_Simon_2001-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
