<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_art" title="Anglo-Saxon art">Anglo-Saxon art</a></div>
<p>Early Anglo-Saxon art, as it survives, is seen mostly in decorated jewellery, like brooches, buckles, beads and wrist-clasps, some of outstanding quality. Characteristic of the 5th century is the <a href="/wiki/Quoit_(brooch)" class="mw-redirect" title="Quoit (brooch)">quoit brooch</a> with motifs based on crouching animals, as seen on the silver quoit brooch from <a href="/wiki/Sarre,_Kent" title="Sarre, Kent">Sarre, Kent</a>. While the origins of this style are disputed, it is either an offshoot of provincial Roman art, Frank, or Jute art. One style flourished from the late 5th century, and continued throughout the 6th, and is on many square-headed brooches, it is characterised by <a href="/wiki/Chip_carving" title="Chip carving">chip-carved</a> patterns based on animals and masks. A different style, which gradually superseded it is dominated by serpentine beasts with interlacing bodies.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg/280px-Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg/420px-Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg/560px-Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shoulder clasp (closed) from the Sutton Hoo ship-burial 1, England. British Museum.</div></div></div>
<p>By the later 6th century the best works from the south-east are distinguished by greater use of expensive materials, above all gold and <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnets</a>, reflecting the growing prosperity of a more organised society which had greater access to imported precious materials, as seen in the buckle from the <a href="/wiki/Taplow_burial" class="mw-redirect" title="Taplow burial">Taplow burial</a> and the jewellery from that at <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Hoo" title="Sutton Hoo">Sutton Hoo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Adams,_Noël_2010_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adams,_Noël_2010-191">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> c.600 and c.625 respectively. The possible symbolism of the decorative elements like <a href="/wiki/Interlace_(art)" title="Interlace (art)">interlace</a> and beast forms that were used in these early works remains unclear, it is clear. These objects were the products of a society that invested its modest surpluses in personal display, who fostered craftsmen and jewellers of a high standard, and a society where the possession of a fine brooch or buckle was a valuable status symbol and possible tribal emblem – in death as much as in life.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards,_Julian_D_1992_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards,_Julian_D_1992-192">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire_Hoard" title="Staffordshire Hoard">Staffordshire Hoard</a> is the largest <a href="/wiki/Hoard" title="Hoard">hoard</a> of Anglo-Saxon gold and silver metalwork yet found<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Anglo-Saxons&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>. Discovered in a field near the village of <a href="/wiki/Hammerwich" title="Hammerwich">Hammerwich</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Lichfield" title="Lichfield">Lichfield</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, it consists of over 3,500 items<sup id="cite_ref-natgeo1111_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo1111-193">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> that are nearly all martial in character and contains no objects specific to female uses.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> It demonstrates that considerable quantities of high-grade goldsmiths' work were in circulation among the elite during the 7th century. It also shows that, superb though individual pieces may be in terms of craftsmanship, the value of such items as currency and their potential roles as tribute or the spoils of war could, in a warrior society, outweigh appreciation of their integrity and artistry.<sup id="cite_ref-Sawyer,_Peter_2013_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sawyer,_Peter_2013-170">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The coming of Christianity revolutionised the visual arts, as well as other aspects of society. Art had to fulfil new functions, and whereas pagan art was abstract, Christianity required images clearly representing subjects. The transition between the Christian and pagan traditions is occasionally apparent in 7th century works; examples include the Crundale buckle<sup id="cite_ref-Adams,_Noël_2010_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adams,_Noël_2010-191">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> and the Canterbury pendant.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> In addition to fostering metalworking skills, Christianity stimulated stone sculpture and <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">manuscript illumination</a>. In these Germanic motifs, such as interlace and animal ornament along with Celtic spiral patterns, are juxtaposed with Christian imagery and Mediterranean decoration, notably vine-scroll. The <a href="/wiki/Ruthwell_Cross" title="Ruthwell Cross">Ruthwell Cross</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bewcastle_Cross" title="Bewcastle Cross">Bewcastle Cross</a> and <a href="/wiki/Easby_Cross" title="Easby Cross">Easby Cross</a> are leading Northumbrian examples of the Anglo-Saxon version of the Celtic <a href="/wiki/High_cross" title="High cross">high cross</a>, generally with a slimmer shaft.
</p><p>The jamb of the doorway at <a href="/wiki/Monkwearmouth" title="Monkwearmouth">Monkwearmouth</a>, carved with a pair of <a href="/wiki/Lacertine" title="Lacertine">lacertine</a> beasts, probably dates from the 680s; the golden, garnet-adorned pectoral cross of St <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert" title="Cuthbert">Cuthbert</a> was presumably made before 687; while <a href="/wiki/St_Cuthbert%27s_coffin" title="St Cuthbert&#39;s coffin">his wooden inner coffin</a> (incised with Christ and the <a href="/wiki/Evangelists%27_symbols" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelists&#39; symbols">Evangelists' symbols</a>, the Virgin and Child, archangels and apostles), the <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Amiatinus" title="Codex Amiatinus">Codex Amiatinus</a> all date from c.700. The fact that these works are all from Northumbria might be held to reflect the particular strength of the church in that kingdom during the second half of the century.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Works from the south were more restrained in their ornamentation than are those from Northumbria.
</p><p>Lindisfarne was a very important centre of book production, along with <a href="/wiki/Ripon" title="Ripon">Ripon</a> and Monkwearmouth-Jarrow. The Lindisfarne Gospels might be the single most beautiful book produced in the Middle Ages, and the <a href="/wiki/Echternach_Gospels" title="Echternach Gospels">Echternach Gospels</a> and (probably) the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Durrow" title="Book of Durrow">Book of Durrow</a> are other products of Lindisfarne. A Latin <a href="/wiki/Gospel_book" class="mw-redirect" title="Gospel book">gospel book</a>, the Lindisfarne Gospels are richly illuminated and decorated in an <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular</a> style that blends not only Irish and Western Mediterranean elements but, incorporates imagery from the Eastern Mediterranean, including <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Christianity</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Produced in the north of England at the same time was the Codex Amiatinus, which has been called "the finest book in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> It is certainly one of the largest, weighing 34 kilograms.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> It is a pandect, which was rare in the Middle Ages: all the books of the Bible in one volume. The Codex Amiatinus was produced at Monkwearmouth-Jarrow in 692 under the direction of Abbot <a href="/wiki/Ceolfrith" title="Ceolfrith">Ceolfrith</a>. Bede probably had something to do with it. The production of the Codex shows the riches of the north of England at this time. We have records of the monastery needing a new grant of land to raise two thousand more cattle to get the calf skins to make the vellum to make the manuscript.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> The Codex Amiatinus was meant to be a gift to the Pope, and Ceolfrith was taking it to Rome when he died on the way. The copy ended up in Florence, where it still is today – a ninth-century copy of this book is even today the personal Bible of the Pope.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BookCerneEvangalist.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/97/BookCerneEvangalist.jpeg/240px-BookCerneEvangalist.jpeg" decoding="async" width="240" height="310" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/97/BookCerneEvangalist.jpeg 1.5x" data-file-width="348" data-file-height="449" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BookCerneEvangalist.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Cerne" title="Book of Cerne">Book of Cerne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evangelist_portrait" title="Evangelist portrait">evangelist portrait</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Mark">Saint Mark</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the 8th century, Anglo-Saxon Christian art flourished with grand decorated manuscripts and sculptures, along with 'secular' works which bear comparable ornament, like the Witham pins and the <a href="/wiki/Coppergate_helmet" class="mw-redirect" title="Coppergate helmet">Coppergate helmet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> The flourishing of sculpture in Mercia, occurred slightly later than in Northumbria and is dated to the second half of the 8th century. Some fine decorated southern books, above all the Bible fragment, can be securely assigned to the earlier 9th century, owing to the similarity of their script to that of charters from that period; The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Cerne" title="Book of Cerne">Book of Cerne</a> is an early 9th century Insular or <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> personal prayer book with <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> components. This manuscript was decorated and embellished with four painted full-page miniatures, major and minor letters, continuing panels, and litterae notibiliores.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Further decorated motifs used in these manuscripts, such as hunched, triangular beasts, also appear on objects from the <a href="/wiki/Trewhiddle" title="Trewhiddle">Trewhiddle</a> hoard (buried in the 870s) and on the rings which bear the names of King <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwulf_of_Wessex" class="mw-redirect" title="Æthelwulf of Wessex">Æthelwulf</a> and Queen <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelswith" title="Æthelswith">Æthelswith</a>, which are the centre of a small corpus of fine ninth-century metalwork.
</p><p>There was demonstrable continuity in the south, even though the Danish settlement represented a watershed in England's artistic tradition. Wars and pillaging removed or destroyed much Anglo-Saxon art, while the settlement introduced new Scandinavian craftsmen and patrons. The result was to accentuate the pre-existing distinction between the art of the north and that of the south.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> In the 10th and 11th centuries, the Viking dominated areas were characterised by stone sculpture in which the Anglo-Saxon tradition of cross shafts took on new forms, and a distinctive Anglo-Scandinavian monument, the 'hogback' tomb, was produced.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> The decorative motifs used on these northern carvings (as on items of personal adornment or everyday use) echo Scandinavian styles. The <a href="#King_Alfred_and_the_rebuilding_(878-899)">Wessexan hegemony</a> and the <a href="#Reform_and_formation_of_England_(899-978)">monastic reform movement</a> appear to have been the catalysts for the rebirth of art in southern England from the end of the 9th century. Here artists responded primarily to continental art; foliage supplanting interlace as the preferred decorative motif. Key early works are the <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Jewel" title="Alfred Jewel">Alfred Jewel</a>, which has fleshy leaves engraved on the back plate; and the stole and maniples of Bishop <a href="/wiki/Frithestan" title="Frithestan">Frithestan</a> of Winchester, which are ornamented with <a href="/wiki/Acanthus_(ornament)" title="Acanthus (ornament)">acanthus</a> leaves, alongside figures that bear the stamp of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a>. The surviving evidence points to Winchester and Canterbury as the leading centres of manuscript art in the second half of the 10th century: they developed colourful paintings with lavish foliate borders, and coloured line drawings.
</p><p>By the early 11th century, these two traditions had fused and had spread to other centres. Although manuscripts dominate the corpus, sufficient architectural sculpture, <a href="/wiki/Ivory_carving" title="Ivory carving">ivory carving</a> and metalwork survives to show that the same styles were current in secular art, and became widespread in the south at parochial level. The wealth of England in the later tenth and eleventh century is clearly reflected in the lavish use of gold in manuscript art as well as for vessels, textiles and statues (now known only from descriptions). Widely admired, southern English art was highly influential in Normandy, France and <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a> from c.1000.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, keen to possess it, or recover its materials, the Normans appropriated it in large quantities in the wake of the Conquest. The <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a>, probably designed by a Canterbury artist for Bishop <a href="/wiki/Odo_of_Bayeux" title="Odo of Bayeux">Odo of Bayeux</a>, is arguably the swansong of Anglo-Saxon art. Surveying nearly 600 years of continuous change, three common strands stand out: lavish colour and rich materials; an interplay between abstract ornament and representational subject matter; and a fusion of art styles reflects England was linked in the 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_first_lines_of_the_poem,_the_Wanderer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/The_first_lines_of_the_poem%2C_the_Wanderer.jpg/240px-The_first_lines_of_the_poem%2C_the_Wanderer.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/The_first_lines_of_the_poem%2C_the_Wanderer.jpg/360px-The_first_lines_of_the_poem%2C_the_Wanderer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/The_first_lines_of_the_poem%2C_the_Wanderer.jpg/480px-The_first_lines_of_the_poem%2C_the_Wanderer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1717" data-file-height="1018" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_first_lines_of_the_poem,_the_Wanderer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first lines of the poem, the Wanderer</div></div></div>
<p>Old English (<i>Ænglisc, Anglisc, Englisc</i>) or Anglo-Saxon is the early form of the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> that was spoken and written by the Anglo-Saxons and their descendants in parts of what are now England and southern and eastern Scotland between at least the mid-5th century and the mid-12th century. Old English is a <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic language</a> closely related to <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">Old Saxon</a>. It had a grammar similar in many ways to <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a>. In most respects, including its grammar, it was much closer to modern <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> than to modern English. It was fully <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflected</a> with five <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">grammatical cases</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nominative" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominative">nominative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accusative" class="mw-redirect" title="Accusative">accusative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Genitive">genitive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_case" title="Instrumental case">instrumental</a>), three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">grammatical numbers</a> (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/singular" class="extiw" title="wikt:singular">singular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">dual</a>) and three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical genders</a> (<a href="/wiki/Masculine" class="mw-redirect" title="Masculine">masculine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminine" class="mw-redirect" title="Feminine">feminine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neuter_gender" class="mw-redirect" title="Neuter gender">neuter</a>). The dual forms occurred in the first and second persons only and referred to groups of two.
</p><p>Some of the characteristics of the language were: adjectives, pronouns and (sometimes) participles that agreed with their antecedent nouns in case, number and gender; finite verbs that agreed with their subject in person and number; and <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a> that came in numerous <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">declensions</a> (with deep parallels in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>). <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">Verbs</a> came in nine main <a href="/wiki/Conjugation_(grammar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conjugation (grammar)">conjugations</a> (seven <i>strong</i> and two <i>weak</i>), each with numerous subtypes, as well as a few additional smaller conjugations and a handful of irregular verbs. The main difference from other ancient Indo-European languages, such as <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, is that verbs can be conjugated in only two tenses (vs. the six "tenses"&#160;– really tense/aspect combinations&#160;– of Latin), and have no synthetic passive voice (although it did still exist in <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>). Gender in <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a> was <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical</a>, as opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Natural_gender" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural gender">natural gender</a> that prevails in modern English.
</p><p>Many linguists believe that Old English received little influence from the local insular languages, especially <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Common Brittonic</a> (the language that may have been the majority language in Lowland Britain, although it's also possible that <a href="/wiki/British_Latin" title="British Latin">British Latin</a> had already replaced it in this region). Linguists such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Coates" title="Richard Coates">Richard Coates</a> have suggested there could not have been meaningful contact between the languages, which is reasonable argued from the small amount of <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>. Recently a number of linguists have argued that many of the grammar changes observed in English were due to a Brittonic influence (see <a href="/wiki/Brittonicisms_in_English" title="Brittonicisms in English">Brittonicisms in English</a>). <a href="/wiki/John_McWhorter" title="John McWhorter">John McWhorter</a> suggests that the language changes seen later in English were always there in vernacular speech and this was not written, especially since those who did the writing were educated individuals that most likely spoke a standard form of Old English. The speech of an illiterate <i><a href="/wiki/Churl" title="Churl">ceorl</a></i>, on the other hand, can not be reconstructed.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The progressive nature of this language acquisition, and the "retrospective reworking" of kinship ties to the dominant group led, ultimately, to the "myths which tied the entire society to immigration as an explanation of their origins in Britain".<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>What survives through writing represents primarily the register of Anglo-Saxon, and this is most often in the <a href="/wiki/West_Saxon_dialect_(Old_English)" class="mw-redirect" title="West Saxon dialect (Old English)">West Saxon dialect</a>. Little is known about the everyday spoken language of people living in the migration period. Old English is a <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">contact language</a> and it is hard to reconstruct the <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">pidgin</a> used in this period from the written language found in the West Saxon literature of some 400 years later. Two general theories are proposed regarding why people changed their language to Old English (or an early form of such): either, a person or household changed so as to serve an elite; or, a person or household changed through choice as it provided some advantage economically or legally.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Over time, Old English developed into four major dialects: Northumbrian, spoken north of the river Humber; Mercian, spoken in the <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">Midlands</a>; Kentish, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> in the far southeastern part of the island; and West Saxon, spoken in the southwest. All of these dialects have direct descendants in modern England, and <a href="/wiki/American_English#Regional_differences" title="American English">American regional dialects</a> also have their roots in the dialects of Old English. "Standard" Modern English (if there is such a thing), or at least modern English spelling, owes most to the Anglian dialect, since that was the dialect of London.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Near the end of the Old English period the English language underwent a third foreign influence, namely the Scandinavian influence of <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>. In addition to a great many <a href="/wiki/Toponym" class="mw-redirect" title="Toponym">place names</a>, these consist mainly of items of basic vocabulary, and words concerned with particular administrative aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a> (that is, the area of land under Viking control, which included extensive holdings all along the eastern coast of England and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>). The Scandinavians spoke <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>, a language related to Old English in that both derived from the same ancestral <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic language</a>. It is very common for the intermixing of speakers of different dialects, such as those that occur during times of political unrest, to result in a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_language" title="Mixed language">mixed language</a>, and one theory holds that exactly such a mixture of Old Norse and Old English is thought to have accelerated the decline of case endings in Old English.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_Old_Norse_origin" title="List of English words of Old Norse origin">influence of Old Norse on the lexicon of the English language</a> has been profound: responsible for such basic vocabulary items as <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sky" class="extiw" title="wikt:sky">sky</a></i>, <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/leg" class="extiw" title="wikt:leg">leg</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronoun</a> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/they" class="extiw" title="wikt:they">they</a></i> and hundreds of other words.<sup id="cite_ref-Shay2008_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shay2008-214">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nick Higham  has provided a summary of the importance of language to the Anglo-Saxon culture:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As Bede later implied, language was a key indicator of ethnicity in early England. In circumstances where freedom at law, acceptance with the kindred, access to patronage, and the use of possession of weapons were all exclusive to those who could claim Germanic descent, then speaking Old English without Latin or Brittonic inflection had considerable value.<sup id="cite_ref-Higham,_Nicholas_J._2013_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higham,_Nicholas_J._2013-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinship">Kinship</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Helena_Hamerow" title="Helena Hamerow">Helena Hamerow</a> has made an observation that in Anglo-Saxon society "local and extended kin groups remained...the essential unit of production throughout the Anglo-Saxon period". "Local and extended kin groups" was a key aspect of Anglo-Saxon culture. Kinship fueled societal advantages, freedom and the relationships to an elite, that allowed the Anglo-Saxons' culture and language to flourish.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ties of loyalty to a lord, were to the person of a lord, not to his station; there was no real concept of patriotism or loyalty to a cause. This explains why dynasties waxed and waned so quickly, a kingdom was only as strong as its leader-king. There was no underlying administration or bureaucracy to maintain any gains beyond the lifetime of a leader. An example of this was the leadership of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A6dwald_of_East_Anglia" title="Rædwald of East Anglia">Rædwald of East Anglia</a> and how the East Anglian primacy did not survive his death.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Kings could not, except in exceptional circumstances, make new laws. Their role instead was to uphold and clarify previous custom and to assure his subjects that he would uphold their ancient privileges, laws, and customs. Although the person of the king as a leader could be exalted, the office of kingship was not in any sense as powerful or as invested with authority as it was to become. One of the tools kings used was to tie themselves closely to the new Christian church; through the practice of having a church leader anoint and crown the king; God and king were joined in peoples' minds.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ties of kinship meant that the relatives of a murdered person were obliged to exact vengeance for his or her death. This led to bloody and extensive feuds. As a way out of this deadly and futile custom the system of 'wergilds' was instituted. The 'wergild' set a monetary value on each person's life according to their wealth and social status. This value could also be used to set the fine payable if a person was injured or offended against. Robbing a thane called for a higher penalty than robbing a ceorl. On the other hand, a thane who thieved could pay a higher fine than a ceorl who did likewise. Men were willing to die for the lord and to support their 'comitatus'; their warrior band. Evidence of this behavior (though it may be more a literary ideal than an actual social practice), can be observed in the story, made famous in the <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i> entry for 755, of Cynewulf and Cyneheard, in which the followers of a defeated king decided to fight to the death rather than be reconciled after the death of their lord.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This emphasis on social standing affected all parts of the Anglo-Saxon world. The courts, for example did not attempt to discover the facts in a case; instead, in any dispute it was up to each party to get as many people as possible to swear to the rightness of their case; "oath-swearing". The word of a thane counted for that of six ceorls.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> It was assumed that any person of good character would be able to find enough people to swear to his innocence that his case would prosper. Anglo-Saxon society was also decidedly patriarchal, but women were in some ways better off than they would be in later times. A woman could own property in her own right. She could and did rule a kingdom if her husband died. She could not be married without her consent and any personal goods, including lands, that she brought into a marriage remained her own property. If she were injured or abused in her marriage her relatives were expected to look after her interests.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon laws">Anglo-Saxon laws</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Law_of_%C3%86thelberht.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Law_of_%C3%86thelberht.jpg/260px-Law_of_%C3%86thelberht.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="381" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Law_of_%C3%86thelberht.jpg/390px-Law_of_%C3%86thelberht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Law_of_%C3%86thelberht.jpg/520px-Law_of_%C3%86thelberht.jpg 2x" data-file-width="643" data-file-height="943" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Law_of_%C3%86thelberht.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The initial page of Rochester Cathedral Library, MS A.3.5, the <i><a href="/wiki/Textus_Roffensis" title="Textus Roffensis">Textus Roffensis</a>,</i> which contains the only surviving copy of Æthelberht's laws.</div></div></div>
<p>The most noticeable feature of the Anglo-Saxon legal system is the apparent prevalence of legislation in the form of law codes. The early Anglo-Saxons were organised in various small kingdoms often corresponding to later shires or counties. The kings of these small kingdoms issued written Laws, one of earliest of which is that attributed to Ethelbert, king of Kent, ca.560–616.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> The Anglo-Saxon law codes follow a pattern found in continental Europe where other groups of the former Roman empire encountered government dependent upon written sources of law and hastened to display the claims of their own native traditions by reducing them to writing. These legal systems should not be thought of as operating like modern legislation, rather they are educational and political tools designed to demonstrate standards of good conduct rather than act as criteria for subsequent legal judgment.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although not themselves sources of law, Anglo-Saxon charters are a most valuable historical source for tracing the actual legal practices of the various Anglo-Saxon communities. A charter was a written document from a king or other authority confirming a grant either of land or some other valuable right. Their prevalence in the Anglo-Saxon state is a sign of sophistication. They were frequently appealed to and relied upon in litigation. Making grants and confirming those made by others was a major way in which Anglo-Saxon kings demonstrated their authority.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The royal council or witan played a central but limited role in the Anglo-Saxon period. The main feature of the system was its high degree of decentralisation. The interference by the king through his granting of charters and the activity of his witan in litigation are exceptions rather than the rule in Anglo-Saxon times.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> The most important court in the later Anglo-Saxon period was the Shire Court. It is of interest that many shires (such as Kent and Sussex) were in the early days of the Anglo-Saxon settlement the centre of small independent kingdoms. As the kings first of Mercia and then of Wessex slowly extended their authority over the whole of England they left the Shire Courts with overall responsibility for the administration of law.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> The Shire met in one or more traditional places, earlier in the open air and then later in a Moot or meeting hall. The meeting of the Shire Court was presided over by an officer, the shire reeve or sheriff, whose appointment came in later Anglo-Saxon times into the hands of the king but had in earlier times been elective. The sheriff was not the judge of the court, merely its president. The judges of the court were all those who had the right and duty of attending the court, the suitors. These were originally all free male inhabitants of the neighbourhood but, over time, suit of court became an obligation attached to particular holdings of land. The sessions of a Shire Court resembled more closely those of a modern local administrative body than a modern court. It could and did act judicially but this was not its prime function. In the Shire Court, charters and writs would be read out for all to hear.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Below the level of the shire each county was divided into areas known as hundreds (or wapentakes in the north of England). These were original groups of families rather than geographical areas. The Hundred Court was a smaller version of the shire, presided over by the hundred bailiff, formerly a sheriff's appointment, but over the years many hundreds fell into the private hands of a local large landowner. We are not well-informed about Hundred Court business, which must have been a mix of the administrative and judicial, but they remained in some areas an important forum for the settlement of local disputes well into the post-Conquest period.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> The Anglo-Saxon system put an emphasis upon compromise and arbitration: litigating parties were enjoined to settle their differences if at all possible. If they persisted in bringing a case for decision before a Shire Court then it could be determined there. The suitors of the court would pronounce a judgment which fixed how the case would be decided: legal problems were considered to be too complex and difficult for mere human decision and so proof or demonstration of the right would depend upon some irrational, non-human criterion. The normal methods of proof were oath-helping or the ordeal.<sup id="cite_ref-Hyams,_P_1981_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyams,_P_1981-228">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oath-helping involved the party undergoing proof swearing to the truth of his claim or denial and having that oath reinforced by five or more others, chosen either by the party or by the court. The numbers of helpers required and the form of their oath differed from place to place and upon the nature of the dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> If either the party or any of the helpers failed in the oath, either refusing to take it or sometimes even making an error in the required formula, the proof failed and the case was adjudged to the other side. It appears surprising to moderns that so important a matter might be settled by one and his friends falsely swearing an oath. In a society in which each was known to his neighbour and in which religious emphasis was placed upon the sanctity of an oath, the system was probably more satisfactory. As 'wager of law' it remained a way of determining cases in the common law until its abolition in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ordeal offered an alternative for those unable or unwilling to swear an oath. The two most common methods were the ordeal by hot iron and by cold water. The former consisted in carrying a red-hot iron for five paces: the wound was immediately bound up and if, on unbinding, it was found to be festering the case was lost. In the ordeal by water the victim, usually an accused person, was cast bound into water: if he sunk he was innocent, if he floated, guilty. Although for perhaps understandable reasons the ordeals became associated with trials in criminal matters they were in essence tests of the truth of a claim or denial of a party and appropriate for trying any
legal issue. The allocation of a mode of proof and who should bear it was the substance of the Shire Court's judgment or doom and perhaps followed known customary rules of which we have no knowledge. Some measure of discretion must have existed in the determining of the outcome of an ordeal by hot iron but result of the cold water and the oath-helping would have been obvious to all.<sup id="cite_ref-Hyams,_P_1981_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyams,_P_1981-228">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon literature">Anglo-Saxon literature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beowulf.firstpage.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Beowulf.firstpage.jpeg/240px-Beowulf.firstpage.jpeg" decoding="async" width="240" height="381" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Beowulf.firstpage.jpeg/360px-Beowulf.firstpage.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Beowulf.firstpage.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="363" data-file-height="576" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beowulf.firstpage.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of the epic <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Old English literary works include genres such as <a href="/wiki/Epic_poem" class="mw-redirect" title="Epic poem">epic poetry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_English_Bible_translations" title="Old English Bible translations">Bible translations</a>, legal works, <a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">chronicles</a>, mainly the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_riddles" title="Anglo-Saxon riddles">riddles</a> and others. In all there are about 400 surviving <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscripts</a> from the period, a significant corpus of both popular interest and specialist research. The manuscripts use a modified <a href="/wiki/Roman_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman alphabet">Roman alphabet</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_runes" title="Anglo-Saxon runes">Anglo-Saxon runes</a> or <i>futhorc</i> are used in under 200 inscriptions on objects, sometimes mixed with Roman letters.
</p><p>This literature is remarkable for being in the vernacular (Old English) in the early medieval period: almost all other written literature was in Latin at this time, but due to Alfred's programme of vernacular literacy, the oral traditions of Anglo-Saxon England ended up being converted into writing and preserved. We owe much of this preservation to the monks of the tenth century, who made – at the very least – the copies of most of the literary manuscripts that still exist. Manuscripts were not common items. They were expensive and hard to make.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> First, cows or sheep had to be slaughtered and their skins tanned. Then people had to decide to use this leather for manuscripts rather than for any of the other things leather can be used for. The leather was then scraped, stretched, and cut into sheets, which were sewn into books. Then inks had to be made from oak galls and other ingredients, and the books had to be hand written by monks using quill pens. Every manuscript is slightly different from every other one, even if they are copies of each other, because every scribe had different handwriting and made different errors. We can sometimes identify individual scribes from their handwriting, and we can often guess where manuscripts were written because different <a href="/wiki/Scriptoria" class="mw-redirect" title="Scriptoria">scriptoria</a> (centres of manuscript production) wrote in different <a href="/wiki/Paleography" class="mw-redirect" title="Paleography">styles of hand</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are four great poetic codices of <a href="/wiki/Old_English_poetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English poetry">Old English poetry</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Codex" title="Codex">codex</a> is a book in modern format, as opposed to a <a href="/wiki/Scroll" title="Scroll">scroll</a>): the <a href="/wiki/Junius_Manuscript" class="mw-redirect" title="Junius Manuscript">Junius Manuscript</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vercelli_Book" title="Vercelli Book">Vercelli Book</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Book" title="Exeter Book">Exeter Book</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nowell_Codex" title="Nowell Codex">Nowell Codex</a> or <i>Beowulf</i> Manuscript; most of the well-known lyric poems such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wanderer_(poem)" title="The Wanderer (poem)">The Wanderer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Seafarer_(poem)" title="The Seafarer (poem)">The Seafarer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Deor" title="Deor">Deor</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ruin" title="The Ruin">The Ruin</a></i> are found in the Exeter Book, while the Vercelli Book has the <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Rood" title="Dream of the Rood">Dream of the Rood</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> some of which is also carved on the <a href="/wiki/Ruthwell_Cross" title="Ruthwell Cross">Ruthwell Cross</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Franks_Casket" title="Franks Casket">Franks Casket</a> also has carved riddles, a popular form with the Anglo-Saxons. Old English secular poetry is mostly characterized by a somewhat gloomy and introspective cast of mind, and the grim determination found in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Maldon" title="The Battle of Maldon">The Battle of Maldon</a></i>, recounting an <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Maldon" title="Battle of Maldon">action against the Vikings in 991</a>. This is from a book that was lost in the <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotton Library">Cotton Library</a> fire of 1731, but it had been transcribed previously.
</p><p>Rather than being organized around rhyme, the poetic line in Anglo-Saxon is organised around alliteration, the repetition of stressed sounds, any repeated stressed sound, vowel or consonant, could be used. Anglo-Saxon lines are made up of two half-lines (in old-fashioned scholarship, these are called <a href="/wiki/Hemistich" title="Hemistich">hemistiches</a>) divided by a breath-pause or <a href="/wiki/Caesura" title="Caesura">caesura</a>. There must be at least one of the alliterating sounds on each side of the caesura.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><i><u>hr</u>eran mid hondum<span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><u>hr</u>imcealde sæ</i><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The line above illustrates the principle: note that there is a natural pause after 'hondum' and that the first stressed syllable after that pause begins with the same sound as a stressed line from the first half-line (the first halfline is called the a-verse and the second is the b-verse).<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is very strong evidence that Anglo-Saxon poetry has deep roots in oral tradition, but, keeping with the cultural practices we have seen elsewhere in Anglo-Saxon culture, there was a blending between tradition and new learning.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Thus while all Old English poetry has common features, we can also identify three strands: religious poetry, which includes poems about specifically Christian topics, such as the cross and the saints; Heroic or <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poetry</a>, such as <i>Beowulf</i>, which is about heroes, warfare, monsters, and the Germanic past; and poetry about "smaller" topics, including introspective poems (the so-called elegies), "wisdom" poems (which communicate both traditional and Christian wisdom), and riddles. For a long time all Anglo-Saxon poetry was divided into three groups: <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A6dmon" title="Cædmon">Cædmonian</a> (the biblical paraphrase poems), heroic, and "Cynewulfian," named after <a href="/wiki/Cynewulf" title="Cynewulf">Cynewulf</a>, one of the only named poets in Anglo-Saxon.The most famous works from this period include the epic poem <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i>, which has achieved <a href="/wiki/National_epic" title="National epic">national epic</a> status in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are about 30,000 surviving lines of Old English poetry and about ten times that much prose, and the majority of both is religious. The prose was influential and obviously very important to the Anglo-Saxons and more important than the poetry to those who came after the Anglo-Saxons. <a href="/wiki/Homily" title="Homily">Homilies</a> are sermons, lessons to be given on moral and doctrinal matters, and the two most prolific and respected writers of Anglo-Saxon prose, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfric_of_Eynsham" title="Ælfric of Eynsham">Ælfric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wulfstan_the_Cantor" title="Wulfstan the Cantor">Wulfstan</a>, were both homilists.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Ælfric also wrote the 'Lives of Saints' which very popular and were highly prized.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Almost all surviving poetry is found in only one manuscript copy, but there are a number of different versions of some prose works, especially the <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i>, which was apparently promulgated to monasteries by the royal court. Anglo-Saxon clergy also continued to write in Latin, the language of <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>'s works, monastic chronicles, and theological writing, although Bede's biographer records that he was familiar with Old English poetry and gives a five line lyric which he either wrote or liked to quote – the sense is unclear.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolism">Symbolism</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Symbolic_anthropology" title="Symbolic anthropology">Symbolism</a> was an essential element to Anglo-Saxon culture. <a href="/wiki/Julian_D._Richards" title="Julian D. Richards">Julian D. Richards</a> suggested that in societies with strong <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral traditions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Material_culture" title="Material culture">material culture</a> is used to store and pass on information and stand instead of literature in those cultures. This symbolism is less logical than literature and more difficult to read. Anglo-Saxons used symbolism, not just to communicate, but as tools to aid their thinking about the world. Symbols were also used to change the world, Anglo-Saxons used symbols to differentiate between groups and people, status and role in society.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards,_Julian_D_1992_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards,_Julian_D_1992-192">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The visual riddles and ambiguities of early Anglo-Saxon animal art, for example has been seen as emphasing the protective roles of animals on dress accessories, weapons, armour and horse equipment, and its evocation of pre-Christian mythological themes. However Howard Williams and Ruth Nugent have suggest that the number of artefact categories that have animals or eyes; from pots to combs, buckets to weaponry was to make artefacts 'see' by impressing and punching circular and lentoid shapes onto them. This symbolism of making the object seems to be more than decoration.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Conventional interpretations of the symbolism of grave goods revolved around religion (equipment for the hereafter), legal concepts (inalienable possessions) and social structure (status display, ostentatious destruction of wealth). There was multiplicity of messages and variability of meanings characterised the deposition of objects in Anglo-Saxon graves. In Early Anglo-Saxon cemeteries, 47% of male adults and 9% of all juveniles were buried with weapons, some of which were very young. The proportion of adult weapon burials is much too high to suggest that they all represent a social élite.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> The usual assumption is that these are 'warrior burials', and this term is used throughout the archaeological and historical literature. However, a systematic comparison of burials with and without weapons, using archaeological and skeletal data, suggests that this assumption is much too simplistic and even misleading. Anglo-Saxon weapon burial rite involved a complex ritual symbolism: it was multi-dimensional, displaying ethnic affiliation, descent, wealth, élite status, and age groups. This symbol continued until c.700 when it ceased to have the symbolic power that it had before.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Heinrich Härke suggests this change was due to the changing structure of society and especially in ethnicity and assimilation implying the lowering of ethnic boundaries in the Anglo-Saxon settlement areas of England, towards a common culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Härke,_Heinrich_1992_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Härke,_Heinrich_1992-134">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The word bead comes from the Anglo Saxon words bidden (to pray) and bede (prayer). The vast majority of early Anglo-Saxon female graves contain beads, which are often found in large numbers in the area of the neck and chest. Beads are also sometimes found in male burials, with large beads often associated with prestigious weapons. A variety of materials other than glass were available for Anglo-Saxon beads including; amber, rock crystal, amethyst, bone, shells, coral and even metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Guido2000_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guido2000-244">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> These beads are usually considered to have a social or ritual function. Anglo-Saxon glass beads show a wide variety of bead manufacturing techniques, sizes, shapes, colours and decorations. Various studies have been carried out investigating the distribution and chronological change of bead types.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> The crystal beads which appear on bead strings in the pagan Anglo-Saxon period seems to have gone through various changes in meaning in the Christian period, which Gale Owen-Crocker suggests was linked to symbolism of the Virgin Mary, and hence to intercession.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> John Hines has suggested that the over 2000 different types of beads found at Lakenheath show that the beads symbolise identity, roles, status and micro cultures within the tribal landscape of the early Anglo-Saxon world.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Symbolism continued to have a hold on the minds of Anglo-Saxon people into the Christian eras. The interiors of churches would have glowed with colour, and the walls of the halls were painted with decorative scenes from the imagination telling stories of monsters and heroes like those in the poem Beowulf. Although nothing much is left of the wall paintings, evidence of their pictorial art is found in Bibles and Psalters, in illuminated manuscripts. The poem, 'The <a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Rood" title="Dream of the Rood">Dream of the Rood</a>', is an example how symbolism of trees was fused into Christian symbolism.
Richard North suggests that the sacrifice of the tree was in accordance with pagan virtues and "the image of Christ's death was constructed in this poem with reference to an Anglian ideology of the world tree".<sup id="cite_ref-North273_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-North273-249">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> North suggests that the author of <i>The Dream of the Rood</i> "uses the language of the myth of Ingui in order to present the Passion to his newly Christianized countrymen as a story from their native tradition".<sup id="cite_ref-North273_249-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-North273-249">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the tree's triumph over death is celebrated by adorning the cross with gold and jewels.
</p><p>The most distinctive feature of <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coinage</a> of the first half of the 8th century is its portrayal of animals, to an extent found in no other European coinage of the Early Middle Ages. Some animals, such as lions or peacocks, would have been known in England only through descriptions in texts or through images in manuscripts or on portable objects. The animals were not merely illustrated out of an interest in the natural world. Each was imbued with meanings and acted as a symbol which would have been understood at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Peterborough_Chronicle_cropped.jpg/23px-Peterborough_Chronicle_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="23" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Peterborough_Chronicle_cropped.jpg/35px-Peterborough_Chronicle_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Peterborough_Chronicle_cropped.jpg/47px-Peterborough_Chronicle_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="297" data-file-height="355" /></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Anglo-Saxon_England" title="Portal:Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxon England portal</a></span></li></ul></div>

<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Anglo-Saxon_settlement_in_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of Anglo-Saxon settlement in Britain">Timeline of Anglo-Saxon settlement in Britain</a></li></ul>
<p><b>Modern concepts:</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon economy">Anglo-Saxon economy</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English people</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Throughout this article Anglo-Saxon is used for Saxon, Angles, Jute, or Frisian unless it is specific to a point being made; "Anglo-Saxon" is used when specifically the culture is meant rather than any ethnicity. But, all these terms are interchangeably used by scholars.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The delimiting dates vary; often cited are 410, date of the <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(410)" title="Sack of Rome (410)">Sack of Rome</a> by <a href="/wiki/Alaric_I" title="Alaric I">Alaric I</a>; and 751, the accession of <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pippin the Short</a> and the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingian dynasty</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There is much evidence for loosely managed and shifting cultivation and no evidence of "top down" structured landscape planning.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Confirmation of this interpretation may come from Bede's account of the battle of the river Winwæd of 655, where it is said that Penda of Mercia, overlord of all the southern kingdoms, was able to call upon thirty contingents, each led by <i>duces regii</i> – royal commanders.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-71">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From its reference to "Aldfrith, who now reigns peacefully" it must date to between 685 and 704.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-140">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Oswiu of Northumbria (642–70) only won authority over the southern kingdoms after he defeated Penda at the battle of the Winwæd in 655 and must have lost it again soon after Wulfhere regained control in Mercia in 658.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-235"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-235">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Example from the Wanderer<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
